Needless to say, this is quite an improvement as far as speed goes.Longevity - The HP lasted around 5-6 hours on normal usage (browsing the web, office programs, watching videos). The GT70 will get about 3-5 hours so carry your chargers, spare battery or external battery pack with you!Screen - It's no comparison since the HP had a reflective 720p screen but this 1080p matte screen is awesome! The viewing angles are great and there is not much reflection (sunlight shines directly at the screen). Luckily, mine came with no dead pixels.Size/weight - Initially, I was apprehensive about purchasing the 17.3" version and was originally going to buy the GT60 but for $50 more, I couldn't resist and I'm happy I chose this model. My HP laptop was about 6-7lbs with the 9-cell battery upgrade and this is supposedly just under 9lbs. Holding them in my hands, I can't really feel the difference in weight between the two but there definitely is a noticeable size difference. I had (HP laptop and backpacks were stolen) this backpack:Case Logic DLBP 116 16 Inch Backpackand it, unsurprisingly, didn't fit in the laptop slot BUT it did fit into the main slot. I'm currently debating on whether or not I should purchase the same backpack or buy one designed for a 17.3" laptop.*Edit*I just bought a Case Logic RBP-117 17.3" laptop backpack and it juuuuust fits in there. It's not a tight fit nor is it a loose it. It's just a bit snug. If you click on my username, you can search for the review.Keyboard - Backlighting is awesome! Yes, there is some color bleeding but since I'm not OCD, it doesn't bother me much. The keyboard, however, is laid out a bit strangely. It's nice that the Windows button is now on the right side so that it doesn't interfere with gaming (never did on my old laptop or desktop keyboard) but the backspace, 0 and right shift button got shortened and the arrow keys are positioned in such a way that you would need to look down to place your fingers correctly or feel for the slightly larger space between the up arrow and the 1 key. My thumb usually rests right under the 1 key but now that space is used for the right arrow key. Lastly, there are two keys for the "\ |" key. The other key is to the right of the space bar and I cannot make any sense of this key.Operating system - Some people hate it, some people like/love it. I hate it and all the bloatware apps it comes pre-installed with. Apparently, Windows decided to ditch the Start Menu because a study of theirs revealed that not many people use the Start Menu key. I use it all the time and I'm quite annoyed that there isn't a factory option to boot up to the standard Windows interface. I've had to "Pin" the Command Prompt and Run programs otherwise I'd have to jump through hoops to get to them (I like having a clean desktop and taskbar). I really don't like the idea of adding on a third party program to get the Start Menu back but there's no other choice.Speakers - Not as loud as I thought given the other reviews but they're relatively loud in comparison to other laptops. My HP had a Beats by Dre sound system/speaker setup and they sounded like garbage but I've never liked any of the Beats speakers/headphones in the first place so I could be a tad bit biased here. The Dynaudio speakers are awesome though! It gives a rich sound from such small speakers.Hard drive - Slow as snail balls! $1,500 for a laptop with a 5400rpm drive? No gaming laptop should be crippled like this. It has two storage bays (this specific model), just give us a 500 or 750 GB 7200rpm drive. Most of us will be switching out to SSD's anyway (Samsung 840 Pro being ghosted).Touchpad - It would be nice to have multi-finger gestures. Scrolling down on this laptop requires you to place your finger on the far right (computer set as right handed) and drag down. On my old HP which also used Synaptecs drivers, I could scroll down using two fingers anywhere on the touchpad and swipe downward.*Edit* It wasn't working before but the multi-finger gestures are working now.Palm rest - It does not get hot at all!Noise - Quiet during normal operation and then sounds like a desktop video card with its fans on max with the turbo fan on. It's a gaming laptop, don't expect to be able to game in a library.Network card - Has better reception than my dual band Asus USB adapter (USB-N53).MSI Afterburner doesn't seem to work on this but the Turbo Fan touch button is right there so no big deal.

$reviewerID.: A3UJVH7WB0OO3C
$prod_ID.: B00EO09NIW
$reviewer.NAME.: Kevin Edwards
$No. helps.: [4, 4] $reviewDate.: 05 16, 2014
$review_text.: I've been wanting something like this for a long time.  GoGoGate, finally gets it right (mostly).Installation may or may not be easy depending on your skill level as well as unique conditions of your garage.  The instructions include a YouTube video that's really easy to follow and for most situations everything will be fine.  However, as you're reading the instructions or watching the video, pay attention to what they are doing and why.  It's understandable that they can't give instructions for every unique situation, so understand what they are demonstrating and use common sense to apply it to your own garage.My (3 car door) garage has the wall buttons nowhere near a power outlet. My wife wouldn't appreciate the controller and the wires being placed near the buttons anyway.  So for me the easy solution was to mount the GoGoGate on the ceiling next to one of the opener motors.  That allowed me to run wires to each of the 3 motors going through the ceiling and into the attic.  Other than the GoGoGate device being visible behind one of the motors, the entire system is hidden.My motor had 4 wires going to it, not 2 (one red one white) like in the instructions.  No problem, the GoGoGate is just a switch it completes or breaks a circuit.  All I had to do was find which of the two wire connections when connected would open the door (the other two were for the lights).The other issue I had was in placing the 3 sensors.  That was hard in my case because there was a metal rail between the doors and the wall.  This meant putting the sensor on the very top of the garage door and doing the opposite of what they suggest, that is the big piece needs to go on the door, and the smaller piece on the wall.  It took some playing around, but it was really easy to figure this out.The double-sided tape to secure the sensors works well if you make sure to clean the surfaces, and don't touch them (oil from your fingers will be a problem).  Then when securing, hold the sensors down firmly for 30 seconds (no wiggling).  Before doing this, tape your sensors in place to make sure they're going to work where you're placing them (with painter's masking tape).  You can't re-use the double sided tape.Make sure to remove the protective film over the phone light sensor before doing the set up.  Otherwise it won't work.  That film should have a notice on it, and I think that's causing a lot of problems.  On the other hand, you don't need to use the phone light sensor.  Follow the instructions and just go to "Find GoGoGate".  The first time you do this, you need to create an open wifi network with your router, but once you've set up GoGoGate to recognize your SSID and password, you're good to go.  Subsequent configurations and changes will work work without having to open your wifi.  For me this is easier than doing the phone light sensor syncing because I need to move a car and get a ladder to get to where I mounted the device.If your SSID has special characters it won't work with the GoGoGate, but that's exactly why your SSID shouldn't have special characters.  Do yourself a favor and don't use special characters in an SSID, you'll appreciate this later when setting up the Internet of everything.Room for improvements:1) Both pieces of the sensor should have holes for securing it with screws.  Currently only the small piece has these holes.2) The "Warranty Void if Removed" sticker is placed in such a way that it broke (95%) on mine simply during shipping and handling.3) The app has a funky web browser inside of the app.  This is better than sending you to Mobile Safari, but it clearly not the same user experience as the native app section.  I'd like to see this better integrated.  One major improvement would be to remove the pop-up that asks you to open the web page.  Instead, just open the web page.  Put the website address in the Settings part of the app.4) Alerts:  It's great being able to get an alert.  My wife used to forget to close the garage door sometimes.  This lets me know this is happening.  I'd like to be able to configure it so that alerts keep coming unless turned off by the app itself.
$review_summary.: Finally someone gets it right (mostly)

$Helpful?.: [4, 5] $review_date.: 11 7, 2011
$review_text.: I purchased the Clearstream 2 to replace an older DB8 and wanted to share my experience.The DB8 was bought a couple of years ago as the US was moving to a digital and I wanted to move away from my Direct TV satellite that could only deliver a low-def picture because of the trees and really didn't work when there was wind /rain / snow etc . The DB8 is a large UHF only antenna that I found quite difficult to point at New York but after some trial and error it pulled in 40+ channels of high def content. I was very pleased - except for the size of the antenna which as I said is large and is also not that attractive.When the analogue to digital transistion happened I lost ABC which moved back from the UHF frequency it was testing at to a High VHF frequency. Irritating. I then bought a RCA ANT751R VHF/UHF antenna that I mounted alongside the DB8. Between the two I now receive the High VHF and UHF channels. I still lost some channels in bad weather but overall pretty good. A little while later I bought another RCA (they are compact and do do a good job) and pointed that at Long Island to get some other channels. I amplify the signal at the roof using a PA18 pre-amplifier for the DB8 and a Radio Shack amplifier for the RCA.Fast forward to this year and I noticed that Antenna Direct had some new antennas coming out that I thought would be better (DB2e, DB4e). I have no experience of these antennas so cant comment on their performance but I decided not to try them as they are still quite large.So, I then decided to try the Clearstream 2. It arrived quickly from Amazon in a remarkably small box. On unpacking I noticed that it hadn't been assembled properly (the hole that the antenna cable was at the top of the antenna and not at the bottom), but this was a 2 minute easy fix with a screwdriver. Swopping the DB8 for the CS2 was also quite easy as the hard work of positioning the antenna on top of the roof was already done.For me the CS2 pulls in as many channels as the DB8 and is visually much better. The antenna is much much smaller, is easier to point (the DB8 was quite directional, the CS2 just needs to be pointed in the general direction and has a broad angle of reception which means for me that I was able to remove one of the RCAs by pointing the CS2 between the direction of the RCA and the direction of the old DB8. The antenna seems also to better equiped for bad weather (being smaller and mounted to a fairly sturdy mount helps) and so far we haven't had issues. In fact during last week's storm the CS2 survived unharmed but the RCA was damaged by a falling tree branch.The CS2 does not replace the RCA used to receive the ABC VHF channel, but otherwise this antenna is simply excellent. Cheaper than the DB8, much smaller and much more attractive.I'll wait for reviews of the DB2e and DB4e but I think I have finished experimenting with antennas as the CS2 and RCA between them do a great job.
$review_summary.: Clearstream 2 vs DB8

Don't cheap out here or you will definitely be unsatisfied with the video!Overall, it's a decent camera for the price, the blurring of slighlty distant objects may not bother others as it does me, so I can't not recommend it based on that. $SUMMARY.: Decent Action Camera, With Some Issues.
$reviewerID.: A3JCZ6ME8QUGTG
$prod_ID.: B00794RDVY
$reviewerName.: M. Carpenter
$Helpful?.: [0, 0] $reviewDate.: 08 15, 2013
$REVIEW.: I have been using this device for a long time, so I feel qualified to go through the ins and outs. If your reason for looking at this is the same as mine, which it probably is, you are looking for a cost effective way to extend a wireless signal from your router that is either too weak or does not cover your full home.SITUATIONI use an Apple wireless router in an 1800 square foot home. The issue is that the Internet connection comes in at a corner of the house, which is very poor planning. If it were in the middle of the house, I would not have bought this device, as the radius would easily cover the whole house. Instead, I send a range to my neighbor even though he can't use it. Roughly 500 square feet of my house has awful signal due to this. I needed something that could reach living room devices, an Xbox, and wireless security cameras at the other end of the home.PROSSetting this device up is a snap. It syncs quickly and gets signal quickly. It is obviously $30, so I was concerned even about this simple concept. When the device has connection, it transmits a solid signal of its own and hands off to the Apple router very well. My Xbox can maintain three bars for online game play. Streaming videos lags a little, but not significantly.CONSThe device, like many extenders, does not truly amplify your current signal. It essentially creates a new network (in layman's terms) that connects to your original. This creates a big problem. If your network is under the SSID ACME, then the Edimax creates a new SSID called ACME001. Any device you have can ONLY connect to one SSID at a time. It typically will not switch SSIDs unless it completely loses the original signal, even if a stronger signal that you have authorized is closer (at least not Apple). I think you see where I am going now. So if I am in one room when I arrive and it attaches to ACME001, it will not let it go, even if I am standing next to the actual router with a super signal on ACME as long as it can even barely see ACME001. This is a big problem because it is slow and it is relaying off an inferior device to get to the original connection. The other issue is that the device periodically stops transmitting to the original. I am not sure if this is due to being idle or a technical problem, but you have to turn it off, wait a few seconds, and then turn it back on. I have to do this fairly regularly. It is still showing in your networks and you are connected, but it is not relaying data anymore. You do not know this until you try to do something online.CONCLUSIONThis is a $30 device. Keep that in mind. I feel that you get more than $30 value out of the device, even with these negatives. I simply work around them as needed and most of my family does not seem to notice or care (although my young son knows to reset it when he needs to go on the Xbox).  In the long run, I think anyone, including me, needs something better. It just can not be consistent to be a powerful tool. For the price though and quick fix, I am not sure it can be beat.

The screen is clear and easy to read, it finds the satellites quicky, it recalculates almost immediately, the voice is better and clearer than on a more pricey model that I tried, and its very easy to set up and start using.It's worth going on-line and reading the manual (it only comes with a quick-start guide), as there were a few features that I couldn't figure out myself, and other features that I wasn't even aware of until I read the manual.I was lucky that one of my camera cords fit the Nuvi, so I didn't have to purchase a USB cord. The unit fit right into the case that I already had from my IQM5The pre-loaded Favorites are very complete and well organized.  One of my complaints, however, is that there's no way to add your own address, then place it into a "Favorites" category.  You have to choose a pre-loaded location to get it into a category, otherwise your location just gets lumped into the main Favorites menu.You probably need a more pricey unit to get more options.I've also found some mistakes in the maps, and a very glaring typo in the name of a pre-loaded favorite, and there's no way to edit this.  Again,you'll probably get more advanced editing features with a more expensive unit.  There is a "detour" feature and a way to add via points, but not a way to save routes in advance of your trip.  I like the turn feature, which lets you see at a glance whether your next turn will be right or left.  I also like that you can turn off the GPS to save the battery when you're looking at a route indoors.  There's also a pedestrian mode and a bike mode, which makes the unit more flexible.I had tried a much more expensive Garmin that had the traffic updates, and didn't like that feature at all. It was very inaccurate and not very useful. I think that's only helpful in metropolitan areas. I don't think its worth the extra money.For a fairly sophisticated GPS that will get you where you want to go, at a really nice price, I'm quite satisfied with the Nuvi 1350.

$REVIEW.: I am using this in a Sony HX300 and so far it has worked very well, although there is definite room for improvement.As I have seen around, many people stressed following the included set up guide. So I did and things went smoothly. Things need to be done in sequence during the initial setup, thus I can see why people stress folloing the guide. It's not simple plug and play.I set up the card to use the internet mode by default because most of the time I use it to transfer photos to my PC. Once I had it set up, it has worked flawlessly. Some tips to make things easy in internet mode on the PC...1) On your router, assign a static IP address to the Transcend card. This way you will always know what the IP address is.2) I downloaded Chrome specifically to use with the card on the PC because Transcend has an extension that allows you to select what you want to download and it will download them all at once.3) I set the homepage in Chrome to be the IP of the Transcend card(I only use Chrome for this purpose) so it starts right up(it will prompt you for the login info but you can save that in Chrome)4) Now this is where the improvements can be made. On the cards homepage, there is a button for "Photos" clicking that does NOT bring me directly to the photos on the HX300. I must still drill down another level to see the pictures. It would be nice if it dropped me in the actual pictures directory directly.5) After opening the pictures directory, I can then goto the Transcend Chrome extension and see all the photos, select them and download them. The shortcoming is you won't see anything in the Transcend extension unless you goto the picture directory in the regular interface first.I'd LOVE to see an actual PC program to do the downloading(something like the camera companies have when you connect the camera to the PC) instead of having to use a web interface.The phone app isn't bad, as it has a little more refinement than the webpage. The only issue I have had is when trying to upload a photo to Twitter on the android phone app(Samsung Note 2 Android 4.3). When I select it, it gives me a spinning wheel for a second then says upload cancelled. It never gives me the chance to initially authenticate with Twitter and authorize the card, like when initially uploading to Facebook did.I've also noticed another bug, when I browse the photos on the camera, it sometimes will not create a thumbnail and I'm left with a generic icon and no way to know what the picture is, unless I open(and wait for it to download) the picture. Frustrating.Otherwise, the download speeds are OK, nothing to write home about, but then again, the pics on my camera are 20.4 mega pixels so that's a lot of size to download.Overall, this works well, but could use some serious user interface improvements to make it a better experience..**EDIT 3/8/2014**I've lowered my score to 1 star. I open Chrome, it sees the card, I see the thumbnails of the photos fine but when I try to download one, it starts then stops about half way thru and never goes any further. I have to close Chrome and start again. I tried Firefox and managed to get the photos to download individually but could not get a 1 minute movie to download. It would start and about about 10 seconds into the download would die and stop.Not sure why it worked with the HX300 but is having so much trouble with the SL1 but since I'm keeping the SL1, this has to go back.**EDIT 3/10/2014**So the issue is wifi RANGE. When the thing was in the Sony HX300, I could connect ok, from various points in my house. HOWEVER, when this thing is in my Canon SL1, the range is about FIVE(5) FEET and that is it. If I don't stand within about 5 feet with a CLEAR LINE OF SIGHT to my router, the thing won't transfer. I was only about 4 feet from my router but didn't have clear line of sight and it STILL didn't work. There is something about the Canon SL1(I see some other with Canon cameras with the same issue) that seems to REALLY reduce the signal coming from this card. I am not sure why it is, it is, so this is going back.

The fact that it includes Denon's Audyssey calibration system, which uses a mic to auto-calibrate the speakers, is insane, given that traditionally this has been a feature restricted to their much more expensive receivers.My ONLY knock against this HTiB package is the instructions. You get two sets: a brief one for the BA speakers, and a big one for the AVR-591 receiver. This complicates things because neither was written with any explicit knowledge of the other product in the package. My recommendation: once everything is plugged in, throw the speaker instructions back in the box. Also, the receiver instructions are a bit wonky at first glance. A quick reference/quick setup card specifically for this package would have been nice.It's all worth it, though. I've watched Blu-Rays, DVDs, and played PS3 games through this sucker so far, and it's simply top notch.

Indoor lighting is more than opening a blind on one side, turning on the dining room chandelier (because it is annoyingly bright) and turning on an incandescent or cfl bulbed lamp in the corner and thinking you have achieved some proper lighting scenario. Lighting is complex at best and most indoor lighting sux for photography, plus you are using a stock lens. I mean do you really think at this price point all your pics are gonna look great? Well only in direct sunlight maybe.The video is touted as 720p and while I don't know about that, I will say that I am very impressed with the detail and clarity achieved in outdoor shoots. I stuck a class 6 card in there as suggested in the owners manual (even though a regular SD card seemed to work) and it is very smooth. I can rapid fire still shots as the data writes very quickly to the card. The feature set is rich and the menu friendly and intuitive. Overall, a great little performer AND I got it for a buck and a half through my employee discount. Oh, and great battery life as well!!!

$REVIEW.: While the majority of photographers have survived using a stack of books or a table in lieu of a tripod, I admit that owning a decent tripod adds more dimension and convenience to taking photographs.  I cannot deny that the VANGUARD ALTA PRO Multi-Angle Tripod covers all the bases (and then some).  I also cannot deny the fact that this particular tripod may be overkill for the everyday "picture-taker" that wants to take the occasional portrait or nature scene.The VANGUARD ALTA PRO is a rather complex tripod that is probably best-suited for "serious" photographers that require a precision-level instrument needed to photograph at multitude of angles.  I avoid using the term "professional" solely because the VANGUARDA ALTA PRO can be used by photographers of any skill level, but the steep price associated with such a precision instrument is logically geared to those with a more serious and dedicated approach to photography (which includes amateurs).Having been quite satisfied with an old Quantaray tripod I've used for the past 20 years, using the VANGUARD was like going from Volkswagen Beetle to an Abrams tank.  The tripod is extremely rugged, and while it is made of carbon fiber, the heft was a little more than I expected.  One the positive side, the solid heft of the VANGUARD provided me with peace-of-mind in ensuring my camera was stable and secure when mounted (probably my biggest concern when using a tripod).  This stability extends to every angle to tripod offers as well ... the camera and the tripod stay firmly put. I do find the myriad of angle options to be somewhat overwhelming in that I will likely never utilize the tripod to its full capacity (think Swiss Army Knife).  With that being said, it also opens the door for additional experimentation.The only issues I really had with this tripod are minor convenience frustrations associated with the legs.  First, I found the twist locks to be aggravating and uncomfortable to adjust.  Second, I would prefer a graphic (numerical) indicator for a quick, no-nonsense adjustment alternative on the telescopic legs.I found the VANGUARD ALTA PRO to be a high-quality and multi-functional tripod that should accommodate the needs of most serious photographers.

However, in my trials it does not compete with the flagship offerings from other makes, such as market leader Logitech C910 or Microsoft Lifecam Studio, which I also own. In fact I think it barely compares with a 2nd tier products like the Logitech Pro9000. Let me try to break it down:Likes:-It is smaller than it looks in the picture and fairly slim and light.-The microphone seems to pick up mid-ranges pretty well, or the voice spectrum. It works well for video conferencing.- Skype certified onboard compression yields better video (at 720p) for conferencing.- Seems to be able to generate a saturated and bright image in low light conditions (though not sharp. See Cons.)-Plug-and-play support in Windows 7 with drivers automatically installed.Don't like so much:-While the pixel count is there, the resolving power isn't. The details are sorely missing for a high end webcam. In fact a 2nd tier Logitech Pro9000 is obviously better in a side-by-side comparison.- The viewing angle isn't quite as wide as most other top tier cameras are able to offer. While it claims to have 73 degrees angle of view, or around 24mm equivalent, it has a fairly average field of view, and obviously narrower than Logitech Pro 9000's.- The microphone's sound, while okay for voice conference, is not exactly full spectrum. There is a general lack of quality of the sound pick-up, without bass and highs in the resultant sound clip. Again it falls short of a 2nd tier Logitech Pro9000 which I pitted it against, nevermind the higher end C910 and MS Studio which this is supposed to compete with.- While subjective, I am not sure if I like the styling which has a hint of 80's design to it. The construction quality also doesn't become that of a high end webcam, with mostly plastic and a pressed sheet-metal front that has some rough edges, and exudes a bit of pretentious cheapness.Other Notes:-The physical buttons aren't terribly useful for me, and it requires HP's driver software to run in the background.- 1080p recording isn't possible without some updated/highend/expensive hardware.- Draws more power than other webcams mentioned here (logitech c910/pro9000 and MS Studio). It will not run from an unpowered USB hub, while others mentioned here could.- The much maligned clamp is almost identical to the one found on the Microsoft Webcams. The only difference is that the cam body on the HP is lighter, and the cable thicker. That may contribute to an easy pull of the cable to topple the cam from its mount. As I lodge it on top of a desktop monitor, so far there hasn't been much problems for me. However, the lightness and relative instability makes pressing those stiff physical buttons on the camera a bit tricky.Final Thoughts:This camera yields subpar results for a top-tier webcam. While on camera compression is a plus, but if you are looking for absolute image quality, the "glass lens" and auto focus doesn't contribute much to sharpen the prevalent softness. The color is saturated and it can look fairly bright, but for a premium webcam (and priced as such), it needs a bit more. The field of view is also decidedly narrower than claimed, and with somewhat unsatisfying build quality. Add a tinny microphone characteristic, the price is just unjustified. For the money, you are probably better off with the competition, notably the leading makes like Logitech and Microsoft. For other than onboard compression and Skype certification, even a relatively dated mid-tier Logitech Pro9000 performed better in many of the side-by-side comparison tests, in sound and image quality.

 I've confirmed it's not the computer or software


I've tried everything from replacing with fresh batteries to using a different computer

The best solution: going back to my old mouse


 Besides, that vibrating trackpad idea never really grew on me either


It's an unnecessary gimmick and another misstep for Microsoft

If people want the feel and response of a track-wheel, they'll buy a mouse with, well, a track-wheel...Out of the box, the Bluetooth connects really effortlessly
 Pairing mode automatically activates the minute you insert batteries for the first time

 You then have six minutes to pair it with your device

 It paired effortlessly with my Win 7 notebook

 I was already using a Microsoft Mobile Mouse 3500 which is slightly larger (mostly in height) and also has BlueTrack technology


 In comparison, the Sculpt Touch is flatter and more aerodynamic

 I prefer the feel of the 3500 which fits the grip of my hand nicely

 The Sculpt feels a little (not much, just a little) more awkward because I can't quite grip it the same way as a traditional mouse, but that may end up being a better healthier benefit
 More on that later

The big selling point is the touch-sensitive multi-directional scrolling bar, a horizontal silver strip down the top middle portion of the mouse instead of a track-wheel like the 3500


 If you want full body touch surface, like Apple's Magic Mouse, Microsoft also offers theMicrosoft Touch Mouse, which has full body touch surface like the Magic Mouse
 This mouse actually works pretty good
 The BlueTrack is and always has been very accurate and reliable for my needs
 I already had BlueTrack in the prior mouse and it truly is remarkable

 They aren't kidding when they say you can use it on just about any surface but some of those reports are a wee bit exaggerated

 See, having the capability to simply do something doesn't mean it can be done well

 Sure, you can use this mouse on carpet and it will work
 Just don't expect the same accuracy as a flat solid surface

 I tested it on medium thickness carpet and my cursor jumped around like MC Hammer at times

 Microsoft coyly says "airport carpet" in their BlueTrack technology page, hinting that maybe you should only use this on flat industrial carpet

 That makes sense
 Flat commercial carpet works much better than the typical thick home carpet


 I tried it on a flat runner and had much better results than on standard carpet


 This is a three button mouse
 In addition to gestures, the middle strip can be used as the left-click button as well
 It also has dedicated left and right click buttons that blend flawlessly into the body
 It's pretty attractive

 The biggest problem I have with this mouse ironically is the gesture strip

 It has tactile feedback that borders on annoying
 I have absolutely no idea why Microsoft thought this was necessary
 And it feels terrible

 Instead of nice reassuring gentle feedback like haptic devices, this vibrates (harshly at times), feels clunky, and makes rattling noises like it's about to fall apart when moderately swiped

 It feels particularly bad when scrolling through long documents or pages
 The actual scrolling motion works well both vertically and horizontally


 I'd love to tell you if the batteries last nine months as claimed, but I will update after waiting nine months, or whenever they do finally deplete, to inform how long they lasted
 It's a nice mouse and I actually think the sleeker design may end up growing on me to the point where I like and prefer it over the bulkier 3500
 When my hand gets tired during long sessions, sometimes I tend to accidentally click buttons


 This mouse causes the bulk of the weight of your hand to rest on the palm instead of evenly distributed along the mouse like the 3500
 It means less fatigue and less bending of the joints as the hand lays flatter.

$REVIEW.: Based on the good reviews on Amazon I bought this camera as a present for my wife. We shot 900+ pictures under various condition using the new camera. We also made a few hundred pictures with the iPhone 4 and 4s, so we could make a fairly objective comparison under various lighting circumstances.The camera looks good and feels well-built. It seemed a worthy successor to our excellent 4MP PowerShot ELPH S400 from 10 years ago. Based on the first impression, we had high expectations of our new top-of-the-line compact camera.Unfortunately the ELPH 500 camera disappointed badly. The picture quality is really poor, essentially ruining our vacation pictures. For most picture types, the ELPH 500 was significantly worse than the iPhone 4s (which has a much smaller lens and 25% fewer pixels). In none of the shots was this camera any better than the iPhone.The main problem is the image quality. Many pictures that our ELPH 500 produced were simply unsharp. The autofocus seemed to misjudge the situation often. Also, the poor focus seemed to be worse on the right half of the picture which might indicate a manufacturing problem unique to our camera. In any case, Canon's quality assurance should have caught this issue. Low light performance was no better than the iPhone, while high-contrast performance was noticeably worse. The only redeeming feature of the ELPH 500 is its optical zoom and stronger flash.The back of the ELPH 500 is a good-looking large LCD touch screen. While the screen quality is great, the touch interface is pretty poor. It does the job, but its a huge step back compared to any smartphone. Most notably are the lack of multi-touch zoom and the inconsistent response to gestures. As the other reviews noted, battery life is inexcusably poor, running empty after about 150 pictures.We are returning this camera back to Amazon. If it doesn't produce significantly better pictures, there is absolutely zero point in owning a dedicated camera that is bigger, more expensive, lacks geotagging and has worse battery life than a smartphone.

This remote can be used with the delayed remote mode or quick response remote mode. It can also be used for the long time exposure by setting the camera to M mode (manual mode) and when the remote is pressed 2 seconds in delayed remote mode, the shutter will remain open until the remote control is pressed again/the second time.Three additional things (general condition to note when using this remote):1. Sometime the camera will automatically cancel delayed remote or quick respose remote mode after idling for a while so you might need to set it again and this is normal (you can set how long you want the setting to stay before it revert to the normal mode)2. When using built-in flash, the shutter remote will only trigger the shutter only after the flash is completely charged (with external flash it will not wait the flash to charged up fully so sometime photos can be underexposed if the external flash doesn't go)3. The camera will autofocus (if it is set to autofocus mode) prior to taking the photo after you press the remote button so you don't need to autofocus beforehandThis is a comparable replacement for the Nikon ML-L3 remote control (i.e. both products are good, and work as it is intended). It is great for self/group portrait (using tripod) and long time exposure (reduce shake vs pressing the camera shutter button directly)Sidarta Tanu
$review_summary.: Simple product, works with most Nikon DSLR, Great for self/group portrait and long time exposure

$review_text.: A little over three years ago, I had grown very tired of migrating data, fishing various different drives for different media, and potentially losing things in the process (which happened more than once).  So I bought he BIGGEST Hard Drive I could afford at the time (which was a slightly older model of this one, but still 1TB) and just put everything on there and started working exclusively off of that from whatever computer I happened to be on. Of course, soon after I got all my stuff in one place (which was easy) I realized, Oh no, all my stuff is in one place". So I got another, identical drive and mirrored everything over to it. I don;t like things like time machine for backup because they don;t preserve everything as literal usable drives... you have to use time machine (or whatever) to get everything back out, which can be a hassle. Also I don;t need everything backed up every few minutes. Once or twice a week is fine for me. So I do a manual copy and replace at set times each week, or on demand, as newer valuable data is added. My disks are over half full a this point so the process takes several hours, but I do it overnight so it's not a big deal.  It's not perfect but it sure is convenient to have one local drive for everything on any computer and the parallel backup gives me enough peace of mind for now. The one risky spot in the whole workflow is occasionally I totally wipe disk 2 right before copying to it (as opposed to a copy & replace over it) so if drive 1 goes during that copy I'm hosed. That's why I usually just to the copy&replace;, It's just that I'm never sure how well that's really mirroring EVERYTHING. There's also risk that both drives could fail at once, but you have to draw a line somewhere I guess. I'm open to suggestions, BTW, I've been tweaking my methods for a long time and am always looking out for safer, easier ways.Anyways, the main point is that I've been working this way for 3+ years no and so far have had 0 problems with it. All drives fail eventually, but usually not without warning and that's what backups are for anyways. So far these drives have been as good and reliable as anything I've ever used. I am looking forward to bigger, faster, and even sturdier ones int he future though.
$SUMMARY.: A solid solution for 3 years and counting.

These don't have quite as effective of a shield as the upper end coax does, but they still shield around 95%, which is enough for most applications.If you are experiencing a lot of engine or external noise, you will want to invest in a more premium cable.  If you're a CB enthusiast who needs the best of the best, you probably wouldn't be happy with this cable either.  But if you just need a decent 18' coax, this is a pretty good buy for your dollar.  The ends stay together well and the coax is fairly flexible and easy to route through and around different things.  All in all its a durable, good quality cable, with passable shielding.
$review_summary.: A decent, basic 18' coax cable.

$REVIEW.: This is a decent back protector for the iPad that fits great with my Logitech Keyboard SmartCover style bluetooth keyboard. Luckily, I am not afflicted by OCD.  The corners are also not quite perfectly fit; they have small bubbles no amount of smoothing will remove at the center of the bubble cutout.Second, the coverage.  It doesn't cover where the SmartCover connects to the iPad.  I was hoping to remove the chance of wear from the magnetic connections.  I don't think it would be hard to provide protection without impacting the viability of the magnetic connections.  It also doesn't cover the actual corners of the device.  In order for the flat protector to fit the rounded corners, the corners themselves are cutouts.  I've found dropped devices invariably land on corners, and am confident if I drop my iPad, it will leave a mark on the corner.  (The four corners are the four actual holes in coverage).  It also doesn't protect the black plastic iPad logo directly, although the thickness of the protection does leave the logo slightly recessed.  Again, I don't worry about putting the iPad down on hard surfaces any more.Finally, the build quality seems decent.  I have two clear creases on the back of the iPad; one where the black plastic at the top of the device meets the metal back, the other three quarters of the way down, I assume a result of peelling the protector from the packaging or repositioning it as I installed it on the iPad.  Aside from those and the rough corners, it looks great and seems like it will help the device hold up much better than it would otherwise.I don't have anything to compare it to, but think the cover is solidly a three-star product.  It's okay, especially wihen the price is considered.  If you're trying to retain resale value of your iPad without distracting from the looks of the device and loathe screen protectors as I do, this device should work great for you.

$review_text.: Today's compact cameras offer (almost) no advantage over the smartphone cameras


SLR cameras and the new generation mirrorless cameras (such as Sony NEX) do provide better quality but are too large and heavy to schlepp around unobtrusively
The RX100 is a camera that is pocketable while giving a dramatic quality advantage over an iPhone 5s


Its beats the larger NEX camera in nearly every aspect

And it is equal or better than bulky SLR cameras (unless you need a true zoom capability).Amazon sent my pre-ordered RX100-3 just in time before leaving on a vacation to Spain

This offered a great opportunity to test my brand-new camera under many different conditions: in dark churches, bright beaches, city panoramas, pictures in musea, selfies, concert videos, etc
After shooting over 700 pictures I am more enthusiastic about this camera than any camera I have owned until now


The pictures are very good
The RX100-3 seems to do most everything right
On top of that, it feels great in the hand as its made of high-quality materials.It shoots razor sharp pictures that are a feast for the eye
The quality difference is immediately apparent when viewed on an retina-display iPad


Thanks to the large 1" image sensor and F1.8 lens it can crank up the ISO without introducing noise, resulting in spectacular low-light capabilities


I hardly used the flash as there is little need with such great hardware


In bright sunlight the built-in ND filter allows to perform some background defocussing.The built-in viewfinder is unique and turns out to be very useful in practice, especially if you need reading glasses such as myself


Popping it out automatically switches the camera on, popping it back in switches it off again
The regular LCD screen can be tilted 180 degrees to shoot selfies, which is a nice touch
Apropos touch: the LCD screen is not

All controls are with tiny buttons, making for s steep learning curve given the many options of the camera
Sometimes I accidentally pushed one of those buttons, and it turns out to be hard to quickly recover from that

This camera is not for you if you are easily intimidated by options and buttons, or prefer a zen/Apple style minimal user interface


To make things worse, the built-in help feature is borderline useless
One has to consult the paper manual to understand what the DRO1 or HDR3 is, and what the sweet spot for each is.The easy to grip ring around the lens controls the zoom, focus or exposure (depending on the shooting mode)


Compared to the RX100-2, the ring operation is much faster, allowing for smooth zooming

The default 24mm is nicely wide-angle, great for most shots

In some situations a slight zoom in is better to prevent the unavoidable distortion from becoming noticeable

Maximum zoom is only 70mm, which makes this camera no suitable for birdwatchers
I do not mind as most of my shots did not require zoom


I enabled the digital zoom to get a little more zoom range

Given the super high resolution the penalty is not too bad.It is strange that all expensive cameras lack GPS geotagging and easy connectivity

The good news is that the RX100-3 has a built in wifi which avoids messing with adapters and wires
 I used it many times to send pictures to my smartphone and iPad
That still requires a bit too many button clicks, but it does work well and does not depend on external wifi network.I did not have the time to read the manual, but I discovered a few other interesting features in the past 2 weeks of shooting:- It has a built-in 'beautification' tool for those hedonistic selfies: skin smoothing, eye-widening, teeth whitening and more
It worked remarkable well even though it seems a bit of a marketing gimmick.- Sweep panorama pictures can be shot either horizontally or vertically
I found the vertical sweep panorama to be an excellent way to get a wide-angle view without much distortion


All panoramas are shot at 1/500s.- I still need to figure out when to use 'intelligent auto' and when 'superior auto'

If seems that superior auto is more creative with HDR (multi-exposure) in high contrast situations.- The built-in flash can be aimed upward to the ceiling, resulting in a softer effect.- The viewfinder can show an artificial horizon that helps shooting straight pictures.- Digital zoom has 3 modes: 'off', 'clearimage', and 'on', where 'clearimage' seems to cleverly adjust zoom range to avoid too much resolution loss
(I have not read the manual yet ;)- Battery life turns out to be quite good
I short over 200 pictures on a day and still had 20% battery left.- The camera has a tendency to focus on the front object and defocus the background in auto-mode

That is great for portraits, but not always what is intended
Forcing a larger depth-of focus seems to require using the A or S modes instead.The RX100-3 is probably the best portable camera currently on the market.

$review_text.: This is a nicely built and well designed external hard drive.  However, the users manual is too complex and the software WD Software is difficult to follow - so be careful to review it carefully.  It keeps track of your initial backup and updated file if you use the WD Smartware software in the correct way.  I found that I was not scrolling down far enough on the WD Smartware Software screen and so I wasn't using the Backup options correctly.  I found that my best bet was to backup only daily at a specific time and to then click on the clock icon at the bottom of the backup tab and choose "backup now".  You don't have to do this, but I found it best for me to avoid confusion and also because I do casual backups from time-to-time and don't need continuous backups.  It can be used on more that one PC/Laptop and keeps the files separately identified - that's another reason for me not doing a continuous backup option.  It also only backups files that have been changed after your initial backup.Also to avoid confusion and problems - when plugged into a PC, it will often popup a message asking if you want to backup "using MS Windows backup" - "DON'T DO THIS" or it will just give you a fragmented backup that is hard to track.  Use the "WD Smartware" only to do your backups and it will track your files and revision just fine.

There is no overemphasis of treble or bass

There is a slight upward angle of the speakers which helps direct the sound right at your ears and not bouncing off your desk.-There is no on/off switch
You turn the volume dial all the way down which clicks to turn the speakers off.-No LED's
That's a positive if you use these in a bedroom.-No bass adjustments or any other adjustments for that matter.CORDS:-The computer audio out cable which connects to the right speaker is only 4 ft


A lot of people may need to buy a longer cable such asCables to Go 3.5mm Male to Male Stereo Audio Cable-The connecting wire between speakers is 6 ft.COMPARISONS:-I did A-B comparisons with theLogitech Speaker System Z623which are priced in the same range


I was very impressed with the Logitech's and you can read my review there for more details.-The Logitech's obviously have more thumping bass being a 2.1 system but you also have to deal with a big subwoofer


The Logitech high-end vocal clarity was superior to the Bose

But this difference is not huge and is only readily apparent if you are switching back and forth between the speakers


Overall, I prefer the Logitech's but would have no qualms with the Bose if I was looking for set without a subwooofer.-The Bose are significantly superior to the Audioengine A2 and significantly inferior to the Axiom Audiobytes.CONCLUSION:-I've always cast a skeptical eye toward Bose products given their history of over-marketing

I was happy to find that Bose has delivered a nice, solid set here at a reasonable price


They sound good but not great and will be perfectly fine for most people as computer speakers

If you are looking for something of significantly better sound quality, you are probably going to be spending a lot more or going with a separate component setup.

$product.ID.: B004R6NSQG
$reviewer.NAME.: finaldraft151
$Helpful?.: [9, 9] $reviewDate.: 11 17, 2013
$review_text.: I must say I was pleasantly surprised by the 'quality' of this kit - It's always a gamble with so many budget lighting kits choking the market, and I have had my share of headaches with third world lighting products that seem to have a life span of 4 hours yet require 12 hours of assembly and modification to make them work.Because of the last minute film and print work I do for my company there is never time to draft up a wish list when it comes to grip and lighting packages, so we opted to go with and use the budget ones and have used  (and thrown out, destroyed or donated) several similar kits over the past few years


I'm happy to announce this is one of the better, if not one of the best kits in this price range

This review being posted after assembly, testing, color temp, test photos and video, and it passed with flying colors.Assembly of the soft boxes is quite easy; They have all of the support rods built into the units so assembly is fast and easy, as one simply just has to push on the base to lock it into place - (it's almost like folding/unfolding an umbrella - A definite PLUS if you've ever spent 2 hours trying to assemble a soft box with instructions in broken English, or via watching a Youtube instructional video) - None of that here - I had them assembled within 5 minutes, mounted, powered and lit


A new personal record, as I figured the assembly would kill a few hours and shave a few years off my life and sanity.PROSAs stated, boxes are easy to assemble
Set up and breakdown in minutes
Light output is solid and holds steady right around 5100K (almost full bright "noon" daylight, but not quite - Not an issue or noticeable enough to even complain about, at least  for my needs) - Diffusion cloth fastens quick and secure with Velcro that is (shockingly) accurately aligned on the boxes, allowing placement without another modification
Great for indoors/small rooms where space/room is an issue
Light stays cool so structure fires and skin grafts are one less thing to worry about with these, as opposed to working with a cheap tungsten lamp that can melt lead after a few minutes of use.Light Stands: Yep

They're cheap


Yet they aren't as poorly made as the others that come with every bargain lighting kit on the market today


I have a collection of cheap stands that are about as sturdy as a few straws and dental floss, and while these aren't quite as bad, they're still cheap, flimsy and liable to fall over from even the slightest sneeze

Okay for interior work - If you plan on using these on exterior gigs plan on clamping or tying them down to secure them


Sandbagging is a tedious option, since the support arms are about as strong as an emery board.Carry Bag and pouches: Actually, the travel bag isn't too bad of quality


Normally the 'travel bags' are about as durable as the light stands, but this one looks like it might survive a few trips before it breaks down and falls apart.CONSCords are short, so plan on using extension cords


The built in support rods on the box that allow for quick assembly are probably also their greatest weakness, as I can see them bending or breaking easy, especially if you will be breaking them down often for storage and/or transport
Not a problem if you keep them set up in a small studio but TLC is definitely needed if you're a run and gun shooter
Like most boxes, these are bulky and must be broke down for proper transport in the travel bag.Perfect for the guy or gal on a budget, or amateur looking to weed out the DIY Home Depot work light kit (not that there is anything wrong with DIY) - Of course these aren't just limited to the amateur guy either - Great for the Pros too

(although a few pro's might detest the thought of being caught using bargain no named lighting) - The light does what it's supposed to do, and while we all might wish we had a cool and hip Arri light package, these will do just fine
Great as simple key and fill light for a portrait or interview, yet versatile enough for the DSLR or Independent guy on set
Throw some color gels on them and tinker with diffusion and shaping/bouncing/flagging light
Remember that while these are bright, they aren't going to transform a dark street or large area into retina searing high noon sunlight.(if you need/want that on a budget, get yourself a couple of used Lowel Tota lights and throw some CTB gels on them)For the beginner:Since they're daylight balanced, using them indoors with standard tungsten household lamps will throw off the color temperature so you will have to white balance your camera accordingly


Under these conditions using your cameras AWB will just give everything a orange/bluish tint so you need to learn how to manually set and/or correct the color temperature (if you haven't already tackled that yet) - Note that these can be used to mimic sun filtering in through a window, moonlight, and with some creativity and gels, firelight, candle flicker, you name it

Add an LED, clear or colored bulb of equivalent wattage and you can establish whatever mood and lighting scheme you feel the scene calls for.5 out of 5 stars - Not factoring in the cheap novelty stands of course.

$REVIEW.: I'm going to post this review under both the Dell Venue 8 Pro and the Lenovo Miix 8 as I believe some people may find the comparison helpful.I will cut to the chase and say that I have decided to keep the Venue 8 Pro and return the Lenovo Miix 8. I rated both of the devices a 5 because they are both very similar and I didn't feel it was fair to dock the Lenovo just because I preferred the Dell. If I could have rated the Lenovo 4.75, I would have.Here is what I like and dislike about both devices:1. Screen on the Dell is better than the Lenovo in my opinion. For example, in the Kindle App for Windows 8, after changing the settings to white text on black background, you get a truly "black" background on the Dell whereas it is more of a gray on the Lenovo. Also, the colors just seem to be more accurate on the Dell. The Lenovo offers a brighter screen but the Dell is plenty bright for me. I was also able to dim the backlight on the Dell more than the Lenovo which was helpful to me for nighttime reading. These are not major issues but it was something I noticed after comparing side by side.2. I have grown to prefer the build materials and quality of the Dell more than the Lenovo. The Lenovo is thinner and lighter which I thought was something that was important to me, but the Dell is much more comfortable to hold. This is mainly because the Dell is made from some sort of rubberized plastic material that is very "grippy". It just feels comfortable to hold. The Lenovo on the other hand is made from some sort of slick plastic and it always felt like I was going to drop it. Also, the Lenovo made a faint "creaking" and "crackling" sound whenever I held it.3. I originally didn't like Dell's placement of the start button on top of the device, and this was one of the reasons that I purchased the Lenovo. After using them both, I have grown to prefer Dell's implementation since I don't ever power off the device. The Start button acts as my "on" button and I have grown accustomed to the placement. I thought I would prefer the Lenovo with its capacitive button, but I find myself having to hit the button at least twice to activate it which is very frustrating. I realize it must be very difficult to design a capacitive button with just the right level of sensitivity, but if Surface and many Android tablets can pull it off, so should Lenovo. Hopefully both companies will figure out a way to engineer a hard button on the front like on the iPad.4. I thought I would prefer having the GPS that the Lenovo offers, but since there aren't currently any good GPS apps for Windows 8 touch devices, I didn't find much use for it. I tried it with Streets and Trips and the program would not recognize the GPS sensor.5. I get slightly better sound quality from the headphone jack of the Dell than I do with the Lenovo. It's just a minor difference and probably just a matter of personal preference, but it is something that is important to me.6. Actual user performance on both devices was about equal for me, even though the Lenovo apparently offers a slightly better processor.7. The Lenovo offers a cheaper case and stylus than the Dell. I have found the Lenovo case and stylus for as low as twenty dollars here on Amazon.8. I was able to pick up the Dell unit for twenty five dollars less than the Lenovo and see a lot of deals on the 32 gb version of the Dell. Something to consider.9. Battery life seems to be similar on both but I can't say I have performed any scientific tests to verify.Here is a list of tablets that I currently own and used as reference points in deciding whether or not to keep the Dell:1. iPad 2. I no longer have any need for this device and find it to be too limited for its size and weight. I have handed this down to my wife and kids. The kids get a LOT of mileage out of it playing various learning and leisure games. My wife mainly reads books and plays candy crush on it.2. Kindle Fire HD 8.9. I ONLY use this device because of the text to speech feature and only when I need to read something technical and/or boring for work. I tried to "upgrade" to the Kindle Fire HDX 7" and later learned that Amazon crippled this amazing feature (you have to buy the audio book now) so I ended up returning the HDX. I may upgrade again if Amazon ever brings this functionality back.3. ThinkPad Tablet 2. This is my go to device for customer meetings as it is very convenient for taking notes with the screen size and dock-able stylus. That said, the device is a little slow for my taste so I'm really looking forward to the new version early next year.4. Nexus 7 (1st gen). Too slow and battery life sucks. Pretty much collects dust now but I do use it occasionally as a controller for my Sonos system.5. Surface 2. Love this device with the Type Cover 2 and use it as a bridge between my home pc (Samsung Series 7 Slate) and work pc.6. Kindle Paperwhite. Love this device for long reading sessions as I don't get any eye strain like I do on my LCD devices.7. iPad Mini (1st gen) Great for what it is, but very limited in what I can do with it. I use it mainly as a quick consumption device and ebook reader. I might end up getting rid of it now that I have decided to keep the Dell Venue 8 pro.Well, I think that is everything I wanted to share. I do want to make it clear that I like both the Dell and Lenovo very much, and would have kept the Lenovo if the Dell wasn't a choice. Thankfully, I have a choice and will stick with the Dell.

$REVIEW.: I have to state that I give the LG Electronics BH6730S Home Theater System 5 out of 5 stars for doing exactly what it says it does, and not because it is the best home theater system available

It is what it is, and it's excellent as a low-end, entry level system.Pros:* Easy assembly and setup* Digital Optical Audio output from TV* Speakers have small footprint (small speakers)* Rear satellite speakers come with longer speaker wire* FM radio (small antenna "wire" included - it works!)Cons:* Cables not included (no Toslink, no HDMI)* All speakers wired (you'll have to run wires)* Commercial network choices limited (No Amazon video!)* Limited number of input/output portsThe only features I didn't thoroughly test were the USB features, and the FM radio

I made sure everything functioned, but my testing focused on playing Blu-Ray and DVD movies in 5.1 surround sound


Kind of silly testing wi-fi streaming of movies and playing stuff through the USB connection since that's all dependent on other equipment (e.g., speed of an external hard drive, your network speed, quality of FM reception, etc.)
Suffice it to say, all advertised features worked
Your mileage may vary.For a small room, setup is pretty easy and took me about 30 minutes from unpacking to setting up and playing the unit

Everything powered up first try, and running through initial setup, including hooking into my WiFi system, was another 5 minutes or so


My only problem was not noticing that the included battery for the remote was totally covered in plastic when I popped it in (doh!).You'll get enough speaker wire included to run everything in a 20x30 foot room


A larger room might require you to purchase more speaker wire, so if you need more speaker wire they just clip into the speakers and the Blu-Ray player so you won't need any special tools or connectors (note: except for the bass which DOES have a proprietary connector)


Two sets of wires were extra long and obviously meant for the rear satellite speakers


The four satellite speakers are fairly small, and can get overwhelmed by the front-center and bass speakers if not adjusted properly


You'll benefit by tweaking your set up (especially if this is your first home theater system).Ok, as a new user of such a system (though I've "used" friends' systems) I was VERY pleased with the booming, theater like experience

My first test was with the Blu-Ray version ofAkirawhich is noted for having some pretty good mastered sound


The surround sound 5.1 experience didn't disappoint though initially I had to dial down the bass which was causing the fire screen on my fireplace to rattle(!).Hooking up my 40 inch flat screen, it was pretty clear that I need a bigger TV
The quality of the sound actually makes my TV look smaller

When I turned off the system and played my TV through its speakers, it was also obvious that I can now never do that again - the TV speakers sound like worthless toys now
It was VERY cool to run the audio out of the TV through the system via a digital optical audio cable (I recommend:AmazonBasics Digital Optical Audio Toslink Cable, 6 Feet)
Your mileage will vary by channel, but I was able to listen to one movie in 5.1 surround sound

Lots of stations with older programming just spewed mono sound through all of the speakers (which was nice, not spectacular)

Most stations came over as standard stereo (excellent, clear, loud).I could go on, but hope this gives you an idea about the system in general
I've had good luck with LG equipment in the past, and the build quality seemed solid
So, this is a low end, wired, entry level system

It's sold and described that way and exceeds my expectations


Five out of five stars for being excellent in its class


My opinion.

$review_text.: I have had mine less than a week, and now it is my favorite scanner

 But I see no issues with that, Uniden has always provided firmware updates with as little hassle as possible.As for the Uniden BCD536HP recall, there is a lot of screeching going on about sending in your receiver and 'receiving someone else's junk' in return


 If you look over the Uniden information, you can opt to receive YOUR OWN receiver back (but it takes longer) whereas if you opt for a quick return, yes, you will receive a refurbished unit with a full 3-year warranty
 My receiver, being one of the first released, falls in the 'recall' series but I have no plans to do so...it has worked flawlessly since I received it in January, even after the firmware upgrade.I'm more than pleased with this amazing receiver and heartily recommend it to one and all!

$REVIEW.: This switch is a good looking, solidly built device. The plastic is accurately joined and has a sort of metallic semi-gloss finish that allows it to blend in gracefully on a desk next to fancy flat screen monitors and flashy keyboards and mice.The proprietary cabling that it comes with is well made. It has color coded speaker and microphone jacks on each end with caps that are attached to the cord with a short tether. This is a nice touch that prevents the caps from becoming lost when the cables are plugged in.Since I got this switch, I moved away from PS/2 entirely and now I run 3 computers via USB only. It took about 30 seconds for my Windows 2000 machine to recognize the device and then the keyboard and mouse attached to it. The Windows XP computers took considerably longer, about 2 minutes.Once everything is recognized, switching between the computers is very fast, and takes about 2 to 3 seconds, depending on how fast you can press the button corresponding to the computer you want to use.Unlike some of the other reviewers, I have had no problems getting my USB keyboard to work properly. My Logitech keyboard has all sorts of fancy buttons and features I rarely use, such as an additional scroll wheel, media control buttons, "internet" buttons, and so forth. These work perfectly, and indeed no differently than when I had the keyboard hooked up via a PS/2 adapter on my old KVM switch.My only other complaint is that they provide a base that gives the illusion of being able to make the product stand upright, but with the weight of the attached cables and the fact that the console ports are on the front, this really does not work very well. Even if you do manage to prevent it from tipping over, you have all these cables in midair and there is nothing provided to help keep them out of the way. I find the unit works best when laid flat.The device is functionally excellent, and looks great. After getting it all unpacked, and seeing how it is well made and how it comes with everything you need, it doesn't seem so bad that it cost $160. The delay for Windows XP and the under-engineered base keep this product from earning 5 stars.

$Helpful?.: [6, 6] $reviewDate.: 05 2, 2012
$REVIEW.: Since the first tablets came out three years ago, have not been a fan on typing in the same surface where the screen is (like the HPs Touch Screens) as your arms and fingers get tired, so had not been too interested in one.  Nevertheless, as I have been following up the tablets and due to a sale on the Internet, ordered a Refurbished Thrive for a very inexpensive price.  It is a 32 GB model with the Tegra Dual Processor and all the thrills characteristic of the Toshiba brand.The Thrive is not the Tablet that Anne Hathaway will bring to the Manhattan office (if The Devil Wears Prada was filmed in 2012) but it's more a tablet that would be used in Iron Man or The Transformers.  It is heavier and bulkier than the Samsung Galaxys and the Ipads (due to the expansion capabilities) and replaceable back cover.  But it looks Avatar alike.The main reason I selected a Thrive vs the well rated Motorola and Asus counterparts was the expansion capacities.  These Thrive Tablets have things used commonly with other electronic devices; USB, Mini-USB, SD, HDMI, replaceable battery and a regular jack for charging, unlike the Ipads which use "proprietary everything" and accept nothing.  Even though I have had this device for a week only, have found some surprising positive features and decided to write this preliminary review.Have been running some tests and here are the initial results.  Battery life with Google Maps, the GPS on and some movie playing is from six to seven hours.  Recharge time is two hours with the factory charger.  The Wi-Fi connects immediately to the Router I use and the Honeycomb (3.2) Android system which is very simple to use as have had a Droid phone for over a year.  The Android Marketplace has now been replaced by Google Plus and the software provided by Toshiba has been surprisingly good.All the applications downloaded work on the Thrive although some (used on the phones) vary considerably when used in these larger devices.  Many of these are similar or identical to their counterparts in the OS Systems but limitations exist.  As of now, the most surprising thing has been the Toshiba Multimedia Player.  There has not been the need to install supplemental VLC or Real Players as the Toshiba does everything in an unanticipated way.Unlike other devices such as the Apple products, the Toshiba Player does play videos in multiple formats, including the classic Audio Video Interleave; AVI that dates from the beginnings of Windows in the early nineties.  This video format allows you to record and watch movies up to an extremely small compression factor and the Thrive plays them like the Creative Zens do, perfectly.The Zens were manufactured by Creative Labs from 2005 to 2007 and unlike the Zune and Ipods, they play every format you may want to record.  AVIs take grossly one fifth the space comparable to other formats.  Thus in a 32 GBs SD card you can store five times the number of movies you may store in an Ipod or Zune.  You will not have aspect / ratio issues as the Toshiba systems compensates for that as does in the Zens.The Thrives audio consists of two mini speakers on the bottom (if placed horizontally/landscape) which have a half an inch aperture on each side.  They can be loud but cannot reproduce the usual low frequencies from regular sized speakers.  But as there is a Bluetooth in these tablets, an external auxiliary larger speaker might help with the lack of bass.Internet capabilities are the typical Google ones; just start typing and you will get it.  MS-Word is being replaced by Google Docs but free software of "Documents to Go" allows you to access al Office features as long as they have been saved in the system.  Skype works perfectly but the tablet cameras are not the best.So far I am satisfied with my new toy and continue experimenting with video compression to evaluate how much can I load into a 32 or 64 GB card.  But, thinking of overriding the Honeycomb System and installing the Ice Cream one in the next month or so.  Let's see how it goes; more to come.  Meanwhile, do not dare to try new things with your Thrive.
$review_summary.: Toshiba Thrive; Multimedia Capabilities

Don't blame the player for your network problems. Highly complicated technology like this will never 'just work' you have to know what you're doing in order to make it work for you.Network setup with Windows Media Center on Windows 7 is NOT for the feint of heart. The player's interface isn't clear on what needs to be done in order to get this to work. It requires some cryptic log in nomenclature we don't use here in the U.S. on our Windows devices. Good luck reading the limited how-tos online. Setting this up is not easy.Once that is set up, this thing will play pretty much ANY video file over my gigabit network. I'm using it WIRED, not wireless. Wired is the only way to insure a clean signal able to feed 1080i (or higher) to this device. This unit plays .TP files, which was important for me because my self-built-DVR records in that format for all HD video. The unit plays this video flawlessly; HOWEVER it fails to skip forward and back in the horribly unresponsive interface. Sometimes it skips too far forward, then when pressing back, it sets the video back to the beginning. So, a DVR replacement this unit is NOT.The BluRay playback is flawless and outputs exactly as expected as long as you set it correctly in the setup menu. Don't blame the player if you can't read the manual or understand that it doesn't know you want 2.1 instead of a loss-less bit-for-bit stream of sound quality. If you don't want to set anything, go to your local brick and mortar discounter and pick up the cheapest player you can find, one that has NO features or setup menus. The sound and video quality is comparably better than my PS3. I have this thing plugged into an Onkyo TX-SR876 receiver with speakers that are way too big for the space, it sounds fantastic and clear.Software/firmware updates have been almost monthly; to some this is too frequent. Several of them have added functionality. I've had the interface to Netflix upgraded significantly since I purchased this device. It's absurd to think our disc player, TV and eventually the fridge will need software updates, but that is the connected world we live in. So far in this case it's worked out wonderfully. Updates have never taken more than 10 minutes from beginning to end. If you don't want to do them you can cancel it and come back to them via the setup menu in the player. It checks for updates automatically when you turn it on.DVD playback is as expected, it doesn't do miracles but they are played in the non-1080p-HD format they provide. On some HD 1080p-capable TV sets these will look grainy in comparison to BluRay and even HD Netflix. That's the way it is, don't blame the player. You are not buying a professional-level line doubling video scaler; don't expect that level of technology for this level of money spent.The physical design of the box is a mixed bag. The fold-down door seems somewhat pointless, just another thing to break. It makes the front sleek looking if you care about that. One good thing about it is that you can add window tint to the inside of the clear portion of the door if you ever want to make the display darker. Or, electrical tape on the inside of the door if you want total black-out without ruining the fingerprint-magnet appearance of the front panel.The unit feels hollow and light, not a bad thing, but don't set anything too heavy on it.I haven't used all the features of this player. I can't review all of the features this unit has to offer. For me, Netflix works perfectly, BluRay playback is incredible, Pandora works wonderfully once set up. The network video playback leaves a bit to be desired but it is headed in the right direction.Some of the negative reviews on here make me wonder who these people are that give the unit 1 star.

$REVIEW.: A battery is a pretty inscrutable item. It's got power or it doesn't. But there are stages of discharge and potential rates of charge and maximum levels of charge which can be realized if the battery is prepared correctly. This charger tells you all sorts of interesting things about your batteries. And one of the best things is that out of four identical  batteries, it will tell you which one(s) are null/dead. That is great. It's a drag to put batteries through a charging cycle and think that they are ready for some work and then they last 15 minutes because one is dead. But which one? The BC-900 tells you the moment you put the batteries in to charge or whatever process you choose.It definitely pays to read the informative instruction book with this item. I was a little put off at first as I didn't want to think that much about charging batteries. But it pays to understand what it will do. This is a battery charger that has a variety of modes and using the Discharge and Refresh modes is important for reaching the potential of your batteries. And the variety of current charge rates means you don't have to fry the batteries to get them charged. Slow and cool is the way to go.I've had mine for about two months and I wish I'd had it much sooner. I tossed three chargers and at least as much $$ as I spent on this one. Plus I bought some more batteries thinking the ones I had were pooped out but they weren't at all. They were fine. They just needed to be "refreshed" which can take a few days if you do it slow but the process really pumps them back up. I'm just using mine for AA batteries for my camera and flash.If you're going to spend the day taking pictures in the boonies, it's very nice to have some confidence that you're not hauling around dead weight. The BC-900 will make that as possible as a battery charger can.I like it.

Requires too much pressure to get it to respond.3. I love being able to remove the iPad from the keyboard when I want to use standalone, but feel like I lose much of the protection provided by the case as I have nothing to plug the hole where the arm detaches from the back of the iPad.&#8226; I would suggest they include a disc that can be inserted to maintain the seal and protection in the future.4. That's fine, however the piggyback cable will always prioritize the piggybacked source so it was always mono sound even if the sound was coming from the TV which had dedicated left and right (red & white) outputs.  I have to disconnect the RCA male end of the cable running to my Airport Express every time I want to hear stereo sound from my TV.2) the other flaw also came from its prioritization.  It will always prioritize the volume level of the piggybacked source, which in my case is a lower volume setting from the Airport Express.  I have to again disconnect the piggybacked cable every time I want to hear the full sound from my TV.If you're very picky about the sound setup, then i wouldn't recommend this cable.  I'll have to deal with this setup until I can a more useful solution and seeming how inexpensive it is, it's not really worth returning.
