$prod_ID.: B005CLPP8E
$reviewer.NAME.: Amazon Customer
$No. helps.: [1, 2] $reviewDate.: 04 14, 2013
$review_text.: Enough has been said in other reviews about RoKu products.  This review is about Distributing RoKu programming throughout your home. This can be done with RoKu-2XD but not with RoKu3.WHOLE-HOUSE NetFlix and Amazon-Prime movies:Add a Modulator and the RoKu Remote Control phone app and you're set to go. Like most homes we have a main TV plus a mix of old TVs in other rooms. COAX wiring is already in the house for Cable TV and our old Antenna. Being able to watch RoKu programming from the Kitchen, Bedroom or where-ever adds a lot of value for us.If this appeals to you, this is a guide to the key points about this setup.1. Get a good modulator. A $58 ChannelPlusCHANNEL PLUS 5415 Single Channel Rf Modulatorwill provide MUCH better picture quality that a cheap modulator. We started with a $28 RadioShack modulator which works OK for old Analog TV's but not so great if tuning in with a HiDef TV.2. Plan on using the RoKu Remote Control app on your phone . . it works great. This is neccesary because the RoKu2 remote is IR so it won't work from other rooms. The RoKu phone application uses WiFi so your phone has to be on the same WiFi connection as RoKu. The only probelm with the RoKu phone app; if your phone rings you can't get to the RoKu controls to pause RoKu before you answer the call.3. One Complication: RoKu-2 does not support HDMI and RCA output at the same time, so if RoKu is connected to your main TV you can't modulate the RCA outputs.  To solve this problem we have a RoKu-3 connected to our main TV with HDMI and the RoKu-2XD is only connected to the ChannelPlus modulator using the RCA output. Of Course you could unplug HDMI cable on the RoKu2 to watch in other rooms but HDMI connectors don't hold well after being plugged and unplugged alot.4. Depending the quality and the extent of your COAX cabling, you may need a COAX amp to boost the signal from the modulator.  This is another advantage with the ChannelPlus units.  The cheap modulators have a much weaker output signal intended for a single TV, not for whole-house distribution. The ChannelPlus modulator will work without an amplifier in most cases.  If you see any "noise" in the picture, get an amp. We used an amp left-over from our Comcast Cable TV setup . . which was just replaced with DishTV.  In addition to better picture quality, the need for a COAX amplifier may further justify the additional cost of using a ChannelPlus modulator, which probably won't need an amp.If you try this  . . enjoy.PS: 16x9 picture format from RoKu will make people look skinny when you tune-in with an old 3x4 TV. If you have a HiDef TV in some other room, you will get a full 16x9 well-formatted picture.
$SUMMARY.: "Whole-House" RoKu is possible with RoKu-2XD . . . not with RoKu3

The lens that stayed on this camera is a Canon 28-135 IS lens and after playing around with this I seriously doubt that lens will ever go back on the camera.The OS works very well. In taking some handheld shots around our manufacturing plant I tried close ups and some distance shots involving various items with text on them. Setup for the LCD ProjectorIt's important to note that if you are using a screen at work or traveling to a location with a projector screen, let alone a white wall, you will want to have some elevation for the projector.For one, the zoom and side tilt is manual. But there is something VERY IMPORTANT that I have to mention.For $50 more, you can get the EX7220 Pro which is wireless and WXGA.  For those who are wondering what is the difference between XGA and WXGA:XGA (1024 x 768), WXGA (1280 x 800), HD (1920 x 1080)As the jump from XGA to HD is several hundred dollars more, the jump from XGA to WXGA is only $50.  So, in a way, you get more bang for your buck purchasing the Epson EX7220.So, while the unit is fantastic for its use.  You would expect it to be much lower than the EX7220 in price.  But it's still a solid unit as other competitors offer a similar projector in specs but lower lumens.  So, you are getting more brightness with this projector, so no worries about the XGA projectors of the past that were very dim.I also recommend if you are planning to run via a laptop with HDMI or VGA, to purchase a longer HDMI cable and VGA (12-15 ft.) depending on your needs (the unit comes with a 4 ft. VGA cable).  I recommend this for anyone wanting to purchase a work projector or for your company.  As I have been in many situations setting up where 4-8 ft. of VGA or HDMI was inadequate.So, I do recommend the Epson EX5230 Pro XGA 3 LCD Projector but if possible, for an extra $50, definitely go for the Epson EX7220 WXGA 3 LCD Projector as the wireless capability and the WXGA will be more beneficial for your business/company in the long run.

$REVIEW.: I pontificated for several months before buying these, due to fluctuating prices and scare stories of fake units being sold on Amazon. I also looked at Ety ER6i's, Ultimate Ears and various Shure units, but can't stomach the cost without a test drive. I may still get one of those sets if these don't work out. I finally decided to bite the bullet on the CX300 since the sound quality on my aging Koss "The Plug" phones has reduced to nothing short of appalling.Firstly, let me say that I am sure the pair I got from Amazon do NOT seem to be fake. They are beautifully made and have the proper, slim profile right-angle plug. I have seen plenty of pictures of fakes on the 'net and these look genuine. The packaging was also top notch.I would not consider myself an audiophile, but I like to think I know what sounds like faithful reproduction and what doesn't. I'm not a bass-head and I mostly listen to 70s-80s rock and pop. I'm an electrical engineer by profession and you won't catch me spending more than $[...] on any piece of A/V cable.My first impression of sound quality was "Ooooh! Tinny!", which surprised me as the packaging promised "Bass driven sound". However, after listening to and playing with them for a few hours, I actually think they're quite good. Remember, I have been using a pair of Koss plugs for the last 3 years, and the quantity of bass on those is ludicrous.[...]Getting a good seal on the CX300s is critical for getting the full range of sound. I have quite large ear canals (probably helped by wearing the Koss plugs) and not even the largest silicone rings supplied with the Sennheisers will seal properly for me. Also, the units are very small and if worn properly (with the cable hanging down), the short length of the driver body does not allow me to push the unit all the way into my ear. I found that the best seal and sound quality can be achieved by inserting the units upside down. The bass is then indeed quite present. Still nothing like the Koss's, but those were a "wall of bass" and altogether over the top.The CX300s have much clearer and present mids and highs than the Koss, but the sounds seem a bit mangled and confused when there's a lot going on in the music. So far I have only listened to 128kbps MP3 and FM radio with them, so more experimentation with a lossless source is needed. I will probably have to re-encode all my CD collection at a higher bit-rate now that I have better earphones.I also discovered (by using the University of NSW's online equal loudness contour applet) that the CX300s have a big response spike at around 6kHz. (I've run this test with all my headphones). I have to knock the relevant EQ slider down about 5dB on my MP3 player to make them sound natural and less squawky.One thing these are excellent for is listening in bed with one ear on the pillow. Since they are so tiny, they don't get forced into my ear like the Koss.I have left them playing an FM radio source for a whole day in case this makes them sound any better (I am skeptical on this) and will report back in due course...

$review_text.: OK, I just received the Slimblade. I have used Kensington ExpertMouse trackballs for about 16 years now, started using them because we needed a mouse that doesn't move when working on ships, but got so used to it and also started needing it for ergonomic considerations.I recently had to convert to Windoze 7 and opted to run the 64-bit version for more memory. Needless to say that the old Kensington ExpertMouse driver stopped working. I found out about Kensington's beta program for the new driver, called "Trackballworks" and decided to test the Slimblade with it, which I wanted for some time, but didn't buy earlier due to its limited programmability and many negative reviews on this issue.The Slimblade is the coolest looking trackball ever and is ergonomically far superior to the latest ExpterMouse, which is way too steep. Slimblade is even better (because flatter) than my old ExpertMouse, which I have to retire, because it is not supported (yet) by the new driver. The ball is slightly smaller than my old ExpertMouse ball, but identical in size to the latest ExpertMouse. Bright red to contrast the charcoal gray housing. That funny little flag sticking out the left side is actually rubber, you can cut it off with a knife.This is an optical mouse, the ball is textured under its smooth surface (looks like a car's "metallic" coating) and two optical units pick up the movement of the ball, which is seated on three tiny little ball bearings (individual metal balls in a seat). Looks like cleaning will not be an issue at all, the bearing balls are too small to pick up dirt and the optical sensors are recessed behind a large opening. The bottom under the ball is open, letting larger dirt fall out. Slick solution!What works out of the box on 64-bit Windoze 7 are the left and right mouse buttons as well as - to my surprise - the scroll function with the ball, which either scrolls up and down in all applications (not just browsers) or, if the vertical is all contained within the window, it scrolls horizontally (e.g. my Explorer windows, which I keep wide).What does not work are buttons 3 and 4 (no function at all, as far as I can tell) and neither does the snap-to-default-button function of the standard mouse control panel.Installing the Kensington beta 0.07 driver gives you more functionality, but, unfortunately, still not the same as the ExpertMouse. Having the trackball plugged in during driver installation immediately shows any added device and the driver allows you to select which settings you want to adjust with a click on one of four icons at the bottom (supported devices are only those that are currently sold: Slimblade, ExpertMouse and two others). Supported operating systems are Windows XP, Vista, 7 (32 and 64-bit) and Mac.With the driver installed, I can configure all four buttons independently. See custom images at the top. Unfortunately, the chording of buttons 1/2 and 3/4 is not available (options are grayed out) on the Slimblade, only on the ExpertMouse.Pointer speed and acceleration are standard fare (albeit without the slick visual curves) as we are used to it on older drivers. The scrolling on the Slimblade needs some getting used to it (I used to twist the ball on my old ExpertMouse as a nervous habit and now engage the scrolling without wanting to) but it is nice, because your fingers don't have to leave their positions - you use the ball to do it. Setting the scroll speed to its highest level enables you to scroll two web pages with little movement and without resetting your fingers.I agree with others who stated that the click is a little hollow and too loud for my taste. The case is made of plastic, feels a little thin, but certainly adds to the looks. Only time will tell how sturdy it is and if it is as flimsy as others describe it.One minor gripe is that the USB cord could be a little longer (it looks to be just under 5 ft), it barely reaches my computer, which is under the desk and to the left of my keyboard (Slimblade on the right). ExpertMouse cords are longer.All in all a very nice product, ergonomically the best I've seen and used and I believe I will keep it, I really like it. The new driver is very encouraging and perhaps Kensington will decide to give us button chording as well as support for older trackballs after release. The driver is fairly stable and has few minor issues.
$SUMMARY.: Very slick device!

$REVIEW.: As a travel writer I spend a lot of time at Walt Disney World, and Canon PowerShot Digital Elphs are the most common cameras I see. Families seem to love them, because they're so easy to carry and operate. About the size of a deck of playing cards, each of these "pocket cameras" really do slip right into a pants pocket, and their rectangular designs make them easy to grip. Best of all, they all take good photos. (How do I know? Because I've actually gotten to use every one of these models. Nearly every day guests ask me to take photos of them with their own cameras, which most often are Canon PowerShot Digital Elphs.)Here's my breakdown of the ten bestselling Digital Elph models. Any of the first six are superb choices:1ST PLACE:SD1100 ISAn update to the SD1000 (see below), this 2008 8MP beauty adds image stabilization, a slightly longer zoom (38-114 mm), another MP of photo size and a small indent on the right side for a better grip. Plus it comes in colors! My daughter has a blue one and uses it every single day. During school days the image stabilization made a real difference in her on-the-bus shots. With that heavy of use hers has gotten scratched up a bit, but the marks are small and hardly noticeable.2ND PLACE:SD870 ISThe ELPH to buy if you want a wide-angle lens. Slightly thicker than the SD1100 IS, this 8MP model doesn't have an optical viewfinder which makes it tough to use in bright sunlight, otherwise there's little not to love. My husband uses one as a comp camera, to compose shots for the travel guide "The Complete Guide to Walt Disney World."3RD PLACE:SD770 ISJust released in May, this 10 MP model is, in a way, an update of the SD1100 IS (see above). I rate it lower because it costs about $50 more and doesn't come in colors. On the other hand, it has a longer battery life than the 1100 and features an improved LCD screen that is very easy to view. As for photo quality, I can't tell the difference.4TH PLACE:SD950 ISThere's a lot to like in this 12.1 MP camera. First off, it's gorgeous. The body, though a little thick, is titanium. An upgrade to the SD850 IS (see below), it has a 3.7x zoom lens and adds manual controls as well as the ability to stitch together panoramic images. The only reason I don't rate it higher is the price.5TH PLACE:SD890 IS(The camera on this page)Want a long zoom lens? Here's your baby, a 2008 10 MP model with a 5x zoom. Also has the new bright LCD screen and all the other 2008 Canon goodies. The most expensive Canon PowerShot ELPH.6TH PLACE:SD790 ISWith the same 10 MP image sensor and 3x zoom lens as the SD770 IS, this 2008 model stands out only for its larger 3-inch LCD display and, as a result, lack of optical viewfinder. The rear controls are different, too, with a flatter design that uses fewer buttons.7TH PLACE:SD1000In my opinion there's no reason to buy this 7.1 MP model. Released in 2007, it's been replaced by the SD1100 IS (see above). The SD1000 is a fine camera but it doesn't have image stabilization, a key feature of the 2008 lineup.8TH PLACE:SD850 ISThe ancestor to the SD890 IS, this 8 MP Elph combines a 4x zoom and 8 MP photo size with an image stabilizer. Every button, dial and switch has a quality feel. Slighter larger than the other models here, it is a little tougher to pop in and out of your pocket, but if you have larger hands it may be just what you are looking for.9TH PLACE:SD800 ISThis 7.1 MP predecessor to the 870 doesn't focus well at its edges when set at its widest angle. Unless it's priced really cheap, get the 870 instead.10TH PLACE:SD750The 2007 PowerShot flagship offers a light meter that adjusts for facial brightness, 17 shooting modes, some first-rate movie features and red-eye correction -- features that were innovative a year ago but are common in Canon's latest models. No image stabilization; no optical viewfinder. Choose a 2008 model instead.

$REVIEW.: I first got the opportunity to really put this camcorder to good use at my two-year-old daughter's first ballet recital.  In a reasonably crowded auditorium, I was able to get seats maybe 15 rows back from the stage.  Without standing behind a tripod, I wouldn't have believed that I would be able to get watchable footage from that distance, but the optical stabilization in this camcorder is astonishing!  You'll likely think there's something wrong with the camcorder when you pick it up -- the internal optical train is floating, loose, inside the body of the camcorder.  This floating feature is what allows you to steadily zoom in on a distant object without translating the shaking of your hands into unbearable shaking of the video.  At the end of the day, we got really wonderful footage, zoomed in tight, of my daughter "dancing" (read: standing still and waving her prop flower at us while music played).The projection feature is certainly neat, but a little gimmicky for my taste.  I can't see a time when this would be the preferred way in which I would showcase the video I've shot.  For reviewing the footage I've just taken, I wouldn't rely on projecting it on a wall, either, lest I think that the focus problems that I might have from the projector were somehow the fault of the video I'd just taken.  Still, it's got a nice wow factor, and I suppose I might find a way to show it off.The lens hood attaches in a quirky fashion, and blocks the 5-channel directional microphone's most important channel (forward).  Not sure I'd recommend using it.The video quality is very good - so good, that iMovie can't import it directly (1080p at 60 frames per second is beyond iMovie's native capabilities).  If you have a mac, I found a work-around: open the drive with the AVCHD file, open that in Quicktime player, and select the clip you want to import.  "File" -> "Save" that clip once it's playing to your desktop, and iMovie can open that file from the Desktop.

$REVIEW.: Decent walkie-talkies, but pricey

 I have bought a pair of these a couple of years ago and they worked well on a 3K mile road trip throughout the West
 They were especially useful when we were in remote areas of our National Park system.As with most walkie-talkies of this sort, you're only transmitting at .5 watt of power, so there's no way you'll get nearly the distance as printed unless you're elevated (like on a mountain) to avoid the curvature of the earth and a clean line of sight


 If you can understand this concept you'll understand that getting 1-2 miles (on the open road) on these is actually pretty good


 You will have to take into context that the level of these units are fairly low quality, so 4-Stars is based on comparing to &#34;other&#34; brands I've used
 If I didn't compare/rate this product in this manner, it would received a 1-star rating


 But in order to get a higher rating, you need to move up to a 1+ watt unit that requires a license to use


So understand this review is like rating Yugo's to Yugo's and rather than Ferrari's to Lamborghini.The build is decent, but lacks weather protection

 I wouldn't trust these things in light rain

 The primary send button is tiny (like pushing the top of an eraser head), which is placed under a 1.5 inch rubber cover, so missing the button which is in the middle is common


 This is probably the most annoying part of the radio


 Also, this rubber piece starts deteriorating under heavy use

 The sound quality is good, much better than what I've experienced with other brands (Motorola, Midland)

 This does lack a battery tray, so you have to use their proprietary battery


 Buying spares is relatively cheap and last much longer than double or triple A's


 I like the NOAA frequencies and the different privacy options


 I've used them in Disneyland and they worked well w/o anyone on our random frequency.Overall, I was willing to pay $20 more for these for the quality of sound and distance


 I've been able to reach a couple miles on a twisty roads and as much as 3 miles on a rolling straight highway
 Of course, these are rare, so expect the 1-2 miles on the highway and a mile or less in metropolitan areas

 In heavily wooded areas, expect well under a mile, but most of the time you won't be separated from your group by that much.


 The build isn't great, but at the end of the day it's the sound quality and distance I need


 The NOAA feature is nice, so in a crunch, I have two extra weather band radios while I'm camping, in the car, or during severe weather at home.

$REVIEW.: If you want to play Nintendo exclusives, this is the latest and greatest. This is for those who want Mario, Luigi, Zelda and the whole gang in their (kids) lives. You're not going to find those characters, story lines and cute/harmless humor anywhere else. I bought this for my kids (elsewhere cheaper this time) for this reason and was pleased that I could still play these games just like it was 1990--same mechanics and similar gameplay, just much prettier. It's pretty unfair to compare this system to the others, PS4/XB1. However, if you have a limited budget for entertainment and games, that's precisely what you might be debating--particularly if it's for your kids.  It's possible this might not be the machine for you. People who get this will likely already own iPads and other tablet devices for some light gaming and perhaps a console like a PS4 or XB1. There's definitely more bang for your buck in other systems, platforms, devices than a Wii U.The big drawback to those other choices like the PS4, is that there's too much mature content on those systems--both in terms of the online connections to adults, the baked-in Ustream and Twitch streams, and also the actual games themselves. So that's a real opportunity for Nintendo. My big problem with our Wii U is the following:Cons:32GB is insanely small for today's standards!!! And access to that memory feels really sluggish...lots of waiting and spinning things. And there are all these cool games on the Nintendo eShop that you can buy and download to your system. But you probably won't be downloading too many cool open world games like Lego City Undercover or Lego Marvel Superheroes because they'll pretty much take up all the space. It's like Nintendo decided to make the shopping basket so tiny you can't put any merchandise in it!  For those games, you'll have to buy the disc and insert it every time you want to play which is decidedly last-gen to me.The game pad is a little insane to be honest. The setup took a long time. Nintendo had or has really big ambitions for the Wii U, like being the centerpiece of your entertainment system, selling and renting content like the other consoles or Apple or Fire TV. But it doesn't want to play with my cable box from TWC (Motorola). It's difficult to find what you're looking for on eShop. The overall UI is tedious, especially if you are used to an iPad or a traditional game controller. I think I saw pics of people using the Wii U with a stylus, but I had no idea they had one slotted into the back of the Wii U controller itself until I turned it upside-down. Honestly it brought back images of Palm Pilots and the late 90s. Not a bad experience with sketchbook-type games of which we've found a few to download. But honestly, it seems really peculiar to me. There are constant moments where I expect the game pad to act like a touchscreen and it wants me to use the controls or vice versa. It can be oddly un-intuitive.The gamepad is iPad mini sized but it's not an iPad. It's not autonomous, it's just a controller sending and receiving from the console. So although it's cool to keep playing games when the TV is in use by someone else, you can't take the game pad too far (15' and a wall)  from the console or it won't work. I find the placement of the buttons and sticks to be sort of weird for certain games causing the player to hold the pad in an awkward way.There's no included remote controller despite it being required for an awful lot of games. I'm going to have to get one of those now. That's fine, but I think they should include it because of how necessary it is to so much of the experience.Pros:Nintendo games! Mario, Lugigi, Zelda and more! The new titles announced at Nintendo's 2014 E3 look awesome! Can't wait for them!!!You don't have to worry about your kindergardeners jacking rides and blowing away hookers on a Wii U! You don't need to worry about your kids learning how to roll blunts from people on PS4 broadcasts on a Wii U! Good clean fun! Yea!!

$REVIEW.: First of all, some perspective: I've spent 10k on high quality audio components before. They were all for my car, however, but I say that to say this: It's pointless to compare this sytem with anything that will cost you more than $400.Now, as far as home audio, it's only recently become a focus of mine. Up until this point, I made do with a Philips system from 2005 that had really small, tinny-sounding satellites and a very small sub that distorted quite easily and could not handle low frequencies very well. Once we began upgrading our TV's to HD panels, the need for an upgrade in our receiver and speakers became painfully obvious.Enter the Onkyo HT-S5400. Initially, I got a factory-certified refurbished unit. However, the HDMI started going bad and I lost ARC and RHiD functionality. Fortunately, I was able to get my hands on a new unit directly from Amazon in February of this year and it has functioned perfectly from day 1.It's a big upgrade over the small cheap 5.1 system I had before. The first thing I noticed was the solid feel to the speakers. My former speakers were housed in cheap composite enclosures that were very small. The Onkyo's front and center channel speakers are large and have a nice, heavy feel to them. I also appreciated that the front speakers had larger enclosures and bigger 5 1/4" drivers, which can handle all the way down to 55hz. Some cheap HTiB's give you the same speakers all the way around and just label them differently. On the Philips, the center channel was just the same small 3" driver that was in all the other speakers, it just had a side-laying box.The satellites on this unit are also large. I also like the solid, real wood black finish (although take note it is just your standard pressboard underneath).The Onkyo center channel is a true center channel, with 2 drivers and a tweeter.For the bass, you have the 10", downfiring, self-powered sub that comes in a large wood enclosure with a large port on the face along with a light that shows red when in standby and blue when in use.The receiver is also a pleasant surprise, with good weight to it and a solid feel.The best thing about this sytem is that it sounds as good as it looks!For the price point, the audio is excellent. Good highs, and midrange. The larger front drivers and bigger enclosures allow for the midbass to actually come through and integrate nicely into the soundstage.The 10" self-powered sub sounds very good. The bass is deep and strong, not too boomy or punchy. It takes quite a bit of power to make it start to clip. For movie watching and music, it's perfect. I can watch a Blu Ray late at night with the volume low and I can still get very good sound, or I can crank it up and listen to it through the patio doors on the porch.The receiver handles 1080p, ARC, and 3D passthrough perfectly with no handshake issues. It integrates very nicely with our Panasonic ST30 panel. This unit also has enough listening modes and audio codec choices to keep us audiophiles busy.We have a large living room with high ceilings and an open kitchen and this sytem is more than loud enough for the listening area.All in all, you can't beat the price/performance ratio of this system. For less than four hundered bucks you get a complete, quality 7.1 speaker, 1080P 3D pass-through Home Theater system that can handle pretty much anything you throw at it.I would recommend this system to anyone in the market for a budget, start-up home theater system.

$review_text.: These are in a class by themselves. All of the usual labeling about highs, lows, mids, blah blah blah..... is unimportant here. I've come to think there is no perfect headphone, except to the listener who is immensely pleased with the sound experience. The HE500 conveys a sound experience like no other cans I've had. I'm totally pleased beyond my wildest expectations. I do have an expensive system though and that may be why they sound so good to me. Being driven by a low-end headphone amp may not yield the results I experience. I will never sell these. In fact, I will request that they be put in my casket when I die so I can take them with me to the afterlife. Do you think I like them? :-) One last thing; be sure and give them at least the 150 hours of burn-in time suggested in the owner manual. Before that, they sounded rather average to me. Some have said they keep improving up to 500 hours of use. Hard to imagine but is fine with me.Update 4.28.14I've had these for nearly two months now. I'm still "Wowed" by them, truly. I recently purchased a pure cryo'd silver cable from Revelation Audio Labs. The cable is not listed on their site but email them to order. They've been making them for years. Bottom line, Wow!!! I thought the cans sounded good before. The RAL silver cable is astounding. I'm having trouble finding words at this point. The pure silver cable takes the HE500 to the next level or two. FYI: The original cable is only silver coated copper. I was told that it's only about 1% silver. Copper really cannot compete with cryo'd silver in the high-end audio arena. So if you want to make the HE500 sound like you spent another $1000 for them, visit RAL and buy their cable. You won't be disappointed. The only problem I have now with my audio is that I want to listen all the time but can't. I still have to work and do other stuff. Darn! :-)Update 7.4.14Purchased a pair of Telefunken 6922 tubes for the headphone amp. They are cryo'd and the detail and overall sound are without competition. $review_text.: This is a simple review and the D5100 is simply amazing! I visited many stores to try different brands and models, including its closest competitor Canon Rebel T3i. I also borrowed from friends to try as well. In the end, D5100 was the winner based on my hands-on experience. You probably have read numerous reviews and got more confused. I was one of you. My advice: visit a store and try cameras yourself.I like the way D5100 fits in my hands (good ergonomics); it is lighter and smaller to carry around (in comparison with other DSLRs); though it is made of plastic (most entry- and mid- level DSLRs are) yet very solid and well- built; its shutter sound is music to my ear; its menu is instinctive and thus easy to get used to; the swivel 3" LCD screen is very convenient; its battery life is relatively long, especially if you use viewfinder instead of live-view most of the time (you should try to do so, as most pros do); its auto-focus is swift and accurate, no "hunting" most of the time; it takes sharp and beautiful pictures, thanks to its newly updated software and hardware; lastly, I like the silver shutter button (I am vain)!However, if you have a smaller budget, the D3100 is the next best thing, and it produce wonderful pictures, too. If you can afford, of course get the D7000 with more buttons to play with and a heavier body to carry around, though the picture quality is about the same as D5100 (same sensor and pixel count) in most shooting conditions.In addition, the kit lens (18mm - 55mm) it comes with is surprisingly good and sharp (tested by "professionals"), which means that you don't need to upgrade your lens gears right away until you are ready to (or your finance allows you to). Believe me, the camera body may become the cheapest asset once you get into serious photography. The lenses are expensive, but they do keep their values. On the other hand, camera body, like cars, depreciates.I am not bias towards Nikon and against others. In fact, I have Sony and Canon, and still own 35mm film Nikon and Pentax. From my experience, D5100 is the best in its class that fits all my needs and most requirements. At the beginning of my search, I charted pros and cons of all possible candidates and ended without conclusions. Then, I realized that, just like buying a car, I needed to "test drive" the cameras also! After all, buying a DSLR is not an easy decision, because it is not a cheap toy (for me anyway) and may accompany for years to come.Furthermore, D5100 can dress-up or dress-down. For a professional, it offers most functions (and some more) that you can fine tune and adjust. However, if you just want an upgrade from a point-n-shoot, it has AUTO and SCENES that you don't need to fiddle anything yet take amazing pictures. Just practice and familiarize with the camera's buttons and functions, you will be very happy with it.Remember, a camera is only a tool. YOU, the photographer, are the one that takes good (or bad) pictures, not the camera. In this modern era, most DSLRs are capable of producing sharp and colorful pictures. The pixel counts, sensors, noises, abrasions, or distortions, etc. can be meaningless, if you don't know how to take good pictures. My conclusion: D5100 is an excellent tool to practice and explore your artistic skills.

The "RJ" in RJ45 stands for "Registered Jack," since it is a standardized networking interface. The "45" simply refers to the number of the interface standard. Each RJ45 connector has eight pins, which means an RJ45 cable contains eight separate wires.

$review_text.: Over the years, I have owned, and used, dozens of USB hubs; from mini 4 port hubs you can travel with, up to 10 and 12 port hubs.  Our notebook computers are not keeping up with the voluminous number of gadgets, and devices, we want to connect at one time, making the Satechi 10-Port USB 3.0 Hub a really great solution.The unit is well designed with a sleek black finish, blue indication lights, and on/off switches. The power adapter is detachable (over 5 feet Long), and so is the USB connection cable (3 feet long).  I was surprised at how small the unit is, at just over 8.5 inches long, 1.5 inches wide and 1 inch high; great size for leaving on your desk or attaching (with the included double sticky adhesive) to the side of your desk.  The hub is separated into four main use points: three sets of three USB ports with each set being controlled with an on/off switch; and on the end, a port capable of charging an iDevice, such as the iPad.  Not many hubs, or even chargers, can make the claim they actually charge an iPad, so this is an excellent feature, including the option to control power to the different sets of ports independently.  These are unique features, not readily available on most competing products.The Satechi 10-Port hub has a power supply rated at 5 amps (5000mA), which is the highest power output I have found among the many hubs currently on the market.  This product is a necessity for charging your devices, and syncing them with your computer at the same time.  While testing the unit, I was able to use all nine USB 3.0 ports at the same time, recognized by my computer; and the 10th charging port for use with a tablet with no slow down in speed or capacity to power.  For example, to max out the unit, I connected 7 external hard drives to it (2WD Elements 2 TB USB 2.0 Desktop External Hard Drive, 4 Western Digital Passport Drives and 1TOSHIBA 1TB Backup Portable Hard Drive USB 3.0&2.0) and a camera, plus my Nexus 7 (for charging).  Read/write speed is just as I would expect, if I plugged each respective device directly into my computer.What's in the box:-One (1) 3.0 USB to USB connection Cable (3 ft. lenth)-One (1)  5V/5AMP power Adaptor (over 5 ft. length cable)-One (1)  double sticky adhesive strip for use in securing to the side of a desk, a wall or even your computer-The 10 port hubThe Satechi 10-Port USB 3.0 Hub is a fantastic product.  It offers more space for connecting your gadgets to your computer, and is a fast, perfectly streamlined, full featured hub.Disclosure: I received a sample product from the manufacturer for the purposes of providing this review. A review in exchange for the review sample was not promised. The views and opinions expressed in this review are my own, and in no way represent the views or opinions of the manufacturer or vendor.

I had tried to set up the camera myself and I did it all correctly except for changing one setting which was enabling TCP for the camera, not both if you aren't sure.  The way to set this up using the NVG510 is to create a NAT gaming profile labeled "Foscam" and use port 8090 then host that port which leaves it open.  You need to be outside of your own network in order to see it at the address which is your fixed IP address and the port number that you have opened for the camera.  You don't need a DynDNS if you have the NVG510 and AT&T; U-Verse, it is a static connection that doesn't change - it is always the same IP address - just write down the number for future reference.  There isn't any support for the Motorola cable modem from AT&T; and people make a much bigger deal out of setting this up because there isn't accurate information available.  You don't need a separate LAN or ethernet or any more network equipment.Now, for the review of the camera itself:  it doesn't seem to work properly at all other than being a remote viewing device.  It takes pictures when there isn't any motion, and most of the time when there is motion it doesn't take pictures at all.  When I turn my light on in the living room, the camera comes to life and starts clicking and taking pictures.  In reviewing the snapshots e-mailed to me, a lot of them are empty of any persons (me) walking by it.  The sensitivity setting is on 10 which is the setting the Foscam rep set it at.  I left it on that setting because I don't have any animals or children that might trigger it.  When I was away and accessed the camera over the internet, I could not get it to pan around unless I used the horizontal or vertical patrol function.  So I am almost wishing at this point that I had not purchased this camera.  I might change this review after I work with it a bit longer, but it seemed to be working at first now it works when it feels like it.  I will try rebooting it frequently to see if that makes a difference.The picture quality is very good and clear, the color is what you would expect from a camera of this price - I am not making a movie so the colors don't need to be perfect - they are very close and the light blue walls and pictures are the right colors.  I was able to look around my living room and see that my stereo and TV were still there which made me feel less anxious while I was away from home for a week.However, I am not happy at all with the fact that the camera is not reliable at all for the motion detection function which is a major reason I purchased it.
